{"parentUuid":"02df65bc-19a6-4df9-b0e5-ec2f09a33118","isSidechain":false,"teamName":"task-slices","agentName":"change-description","userType":"external","cwd":"/home/jwilger/projects/todo_list-worktrees/change-description","sessionId":"052e2b27-b814-496a-bc97-1e35a650e85c","version":"2.1.37","gitBranch":"slice/change-description","slug":"misty-bouncing-charm","type":"progress","data":{"message":{"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01FcDzuFG2Jm5QjstcVKsBHy","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→# Architecture\n     2→\n     3→> This is the authoritative source for the current architecture of the Todo List application.\n     4→> For the rationale behind each decision, see the ADR PRs labeled `adr` on GitHub.\n     5→\n     6→## Technology Stack\n     7→\n     8→### Event Sourcing Framework: Commanded\n     9→\n    10→The application uses [Commanded](https://github.com/commanded/commanded) as its\n    11→CQRS/ES framework. Commanded provides the full command dispatch pipeline,\n    12→aggregate lifecycle management, event persistence, and projection infrastructure.\n    13→\n    14→Key components from the Commanded ecosystem:\n    15→\n    16→- **commanded** -- Core framework (command router, aggregate callbacks, middleware)\n    17→- **commanded_eventstore_adapter** -- Bridges Commanded to the EventStore backend\n    18→- **commanded_ecto_projections** -- Ecto-based read model projections\n    19→- **commanded_boilerplate** -- Ergonomic macros for aggregate-per-command pattern\n    20→  (GitHub: `jwilger/commanded_boilerplate`)\n    21→\n    22→See \"Domain Architecture\" below for how commands and aggregates are structured.\n    23→\n    24→### Event Store Backend: PostgreSQL (EventStore) + In-Memory for Tests\n    25→\n    26→Events are durably stored in PostgreSQL using the\n    27→[eventstore](https://github.com/commanded/eventstore) library with the\n    28→`commanded_eventstore_adapter`. This requires a dedicated PostgreSQL database\n    29→separate from the main Ecto application database.\n    30→\n    31→**Two PostgreSQL databases** (same docker-compose instance):\n    32→\n    33→- **Application database** (Ecto) -- Projections / read models, standard Phoenix data\n    34→- **Event store database** (EventStore) -- Append-only event streams\n    35→\n    36→**Test environment:** Tests use Commanded's built-in in-memory event store adapter.\n    37→This keeps tests fast (no database I/O), isolated (no shared state between tests),\n    38→and free of infrastructure dependencies.\n    39→\n    40→### Projection Mechanism: commanded_ecto_projections\n    41→\n    42→Read models are built using\n    43→[commanded_ecto_projections](https://github.com/commanded/commanded-ecto-projections).\n    44→Each read model has a corresponding Projector module that uses\n    45→`Commanded.Projections.Ecto` and implements `project/2` callbacks for each event\n    46→type it handles.\n    47→\n    48→**Two projectors:**\n    49→\n    50→- **TaskProjector** -- Projects events into an Ecto schema used by both the\n    51→  ActiveTaskList and CompletedTaskList read models\n    52→\n    53→Projection tables live in the **application database** (Ecto), not the event store\n    54→database. The library handles:\n    55→\n    56→- **Idempotency** -- Tracks which events have been processed, preventing duplicates\n    57→- **Error handling** -- Provides configurable error/retry behaviour\n    58→- **Subscription management** -- Integrates with Commanded's event subscription system\n    59→\n    60→### UUID Generation: UUIDv7 (uuidv7 hex package)\n    61→\n    62→Task identifiers use UUID v7 via the\n    63→[uuidv7](https://hex.pm/packages/uuidv7) hex package (v1.0.0). UUID v7\n    64→(RFC 9562) embeds a Unix timestamp in the high bits, producing identifiers that\n    65→are monotonically increasing and time-sortable while remaining globally unique.\n    66→\n    67→The package includes an `UUIDv7` Ecto type for seamless schema integration.\n    68→\n    69→**Generation strategy:** Task IDs are generated by the context module\n    70→(`TodoList.Tasks`) and passed on the command struct. The aggregate does not\n    71→generate IDs internally. This design enables tests to supply deterministic IDs\n    72→without mocking.\n    73→\n    74→## Domain Architecture\n    75→\n    76→### Aggregate Design: Aggregate-per-Command with commanded_boilerplate\n    77→\n    78→The application uses the **aggregate-per-command** pattern via the\n    79→[commanded_boilerplate](https://github.com/jwilger/commanded_boilerplate) library.\n    80→Each command module defines its own inline aggregate containing **only** the state\n    81→fields needed for that specific command's decision-making. There is no shared\n    82→\"Task\" aggregate module.\n    83→\n    84→**Key aspects:**\n    85→\n    86→- `use Commanded.Boilerplate.Command, identifier: :task_id` -- Sets up the command\n    87→  module with routing, validation middleware, and authorization middleware\n    88→- `inputs do ... end` -- Defines the command's input fields as a typed struct\n    89→- `aggregate do ... end` -- Defines the command's inline aggregate with only\n    90→  relevant fields and `apply/2` callbacks for event application\n    91→- `handle/2` -- The command handler callback that receives aggregate state and\n    92→  command, returns events or errors\n    93→- `register_command/2` -- Auto-registers the command with Commanded's router\n    94→\n    95→**Domain logic** lives directly in the command module's `handle/2` callback.\n    96→There is no separate domain layer and no `lib/todo_list/domain/` directory.\n    97→\n    98→**Aggregate lifecycle:** `DefaultLifespan` stops aggregate processes immediately\n    99→after command execution (reliability over performance). Each command execution\n   100→loads only the state it needs.\n   101→\n   102→**Authorization:** The boilerplate includes authorization middleware. For this\n   103→single-user app with no authentication, the `authorize/2` callback returns `:ok`\n   104→unconditionally.\n   105→\n   106→**Read model queries:** The boilerplate also provides `Commanded.Boilerplate.Query`\n   107→for read model access with validation and Repo integration.\n   108→\n   109→**Maintenance:** The `commanded_boilerplate` library is owned by the project\n   110→maintainer. If compatibility issues arise with newer Commanded versions, it can be\n   111→cloned locally at `../commanded_boilerplate/` for updates.\n   112→\n   113→### Domain Types: Semantic Types via newt\n   114→\n   115→The application uses **dedicated semantic domain types** built with the\n   116→[newt](https://hex.pm/packages/newt) library (v9.0.0). Newt is already a\n   117→transitive dependency via `commanded_boilerplate`; the library is owned by the\n   118→project maintainer (GitHub: `jwilger/newt`).\n   119→\n   120→**Naming convention -- domain-semantic, not structural:** Types are named after\n   121→what they represent in the domain, not their structural shape.\n   122→\n   123→- `TodoList.TaskDescription` -- wraps `String.t()`, validates non-empty /\n   124→  non-blank, trims whitespace\n   125→- `TodoList.TaskId` -- wraps `String.t()`, validates UUIDv7 format\n   126→- `TodoList.TaskPosition` -- wraps `integer()`, validates positive integer\n   127→\n   128→**How newt works:**\n   129→\n   130→- `use Newt, type: <inner_type>` -- Creates a wrapped struct with `new/1`,\n   131→  `new!/1`, `unwrap/1`\n   132→- `validate/1` callback -- Override to add domain-specific validation logic\n   133→- Auto-generated `Ectotype` module (e.g., `TodoList.TaskDescription.Ectotype`)\n   134→  implementing `Ecto.Type` for seamless Ecto schema integration\n   135→- Protocol implementations for `Jason`, `Phoenix.Param`, `Phoenix.HTML.Safe`,\n   136→  `Inspect`, `String.Chars`\n   137→- `type!/2` guard macro for pattern matching\n   138→\n   139→**Parse at boundaries, trust internally:** Raw strings and integers enter the\n   140→system at command boundaries (e.g., from LiveView forms). Commands parse them\n   141→into domain types using `new/1`. Once parsed, domain types flow through events,\n   142→aggregates, and projections without re-validation. This ensures invalid data\n   143→never propagates past the boundary.\n   144→\n   145→**Ecto integration:** Each newt type auto-generates an `Ectotype` module that\n   146→implements `Ecto.Type`, so projection schemas use field types like\n   147→`TodoList.TaskDescription.Ectotype` directly.\n   148→\n   149→**Maintenance:** The `newt` library is owned by the project maintainer. If\n   150→issues arise, it can be cloned locally at `../newt/` for updates.\n   151→\n   152→### Context Module API: TodoList.Tasks\n   153→\n   154→The context module `TodoList.Tasks` is the **single API boundary** between the\n   155→web layer (LiveView) and the domain layer (commands, aggregates, projections).\n   156→LiveView ONLY talks to the context module -- never to Commanded directly.\n   157→\n   158→**Command functions** wrap command dispatch. Each function constructs the command\n   159→struct, dispatches it via Commanded, and returns a clean result:\n   160→\n   161→- `add_task(description)` -- Generates a `TaskId` via UUIDv7, builds an\n   162→  `AddTask` command, dispatches it. Returns `{:ok, task_id}` or\n   163→  `{:error, reason}`.\n   164→- `complete_task(task_id)` -- Returns `:ok` or `{:error, reason}`.\n   165→- `reopen_task(task_id)` -- Returns `:ok` or `{:error, reason}`.\n   166→- `change_task_description(task_id, new_description)` -- Returns `:ok` or\n   167→  `{:error, reason}`.\n   168→- `reprioritize_task(task_id, new_position)` -- Returns `:ok` or\n   169→  `{:error, reason}`.\n   170→- `delete_task(task_id)` -- Returns `:ok` or `{:error, reason}`.\n   171→\n   172→**Query functions** wrap read model access using\n   173→`Commanded.Boilerplate.Query` internally:\n   174→\n   175→- `active_tasks()` -- Returns the list of active (non-completed) tasks ordered\n   176→  by position.\n   177→- `completed_tasks()` -- Returns the list of completed tasks ordered by\n   178→  completion time descending.\n   179→\n   180→**Responsibilities of the context module:**\n   181→\n   182→- **UUID generation** -- Creates `TaskId` values via UUIDv7 for new tasks\n   183→- **Command construction** -- Builds command structs from function arguments\n   184→- **Command dispatch** -- Sends commands through Commanded's pipeline\n   185→- **Response transformation** -- Converts Commanded dispatch results into clean\n   186→  `{:ok, result}` / `{:error, reason}` tuples\n   187→- **Query execution** -- Fetches projection data via the boilerplate's Query\n   188→  module\n   189→\n   190→**Testing benefit:** LiveView tests can mock or stub the context module rather\n   191→than mocking Commanded internals, keeping integration tests focused on UI\n   192→behaviour.\n   193→\n   194→## Integration Patterns\n   195→\n   196→### LiveView Integration: Streams + Projection Subscriptions (Hybrid)\n   197→\n   198→LiveView uses a **hybrid** approach: **streams** for the main task lists and\n   199→**direct reads** for one-off queries or detail views.\n   200→\n   201→**Streams for lists:** The ActiveTaskList and CompletedTaskList are rendered\n   202→using LiveView's `stream/3`. Streams efficiently track insertions, updates, and\n   203→deletions -- only changed items are sent to the client.\n   204→\n   205→- Parent elements use `phx-update=\"stream\"`\n   206→- Each streamed item has a DOM ID (e.g., `\"tasks-#{task.id}\"`)\n   207→- Streams are **not enumerable** -- empty states and counts use separate assigns\n   208→\n   209→**Projection subscriptions for real-time updates:** Projectors broadcast\n   210→changes via Phoenix PubSub after processing events. LiveView subscribes to\n   211→these broadcasts on mount and updates streams accordingly.\n   212→\n   213→**Lifecycle:**\n   214→\n   215→1. **Mount:** Subscribe to projection update topics via Phoenix PubSub; stream\n   216→   initial data from `TodoList.Tasks.active_tasks()` or\n   217→   `TodoList.Tasks.completed_tasks()`\n   218→2. **User action:** Event handler calls a context command function (e.g.,\n   219→   `TodoList.Tasks.complete_task(task_id)`)\n   220→3. **Projection update:** The projector processes the resulting event, updates\n   221→   the database, and broadcasts via PubSub\n   222→4. **Stream update:** LiveView receives the PubSub message in `handle_info/2`\n   223→   and calls `stream_insert/4`, `stream_delete/3`, etc.\n   224→\n   225→**Key details:**\n   226→\n   227→- Commands dispatch through the context module (never directly from LiveView)\n   228→- The LiveView does **not** re-fetch the full list after each command; the\n   229→  PubSub subscription delivers granular updates\n   230→- For empty states and aggregate counts (e.g., `completed_count`), use separate\n   231→  assigns updated alongside stream operations\n   232→- This is a single-user app, but the subscription pattern keeps the architecture\n   233→  clean, testable, and consistent with how a multi-user app would work\n   234→\n   235→## Cross-Cutting Concerns\n   236→\n   237→### Testing Strategy: Three-Layer Pyramid\n   238→\n   239→Tests are organized in three layers, from fastest/most-isolated to\n   240→slowest/most-integrated. GWT (Given-When-Then) scenarios from the event model\n   241→translate directly into test cases.\n   242→\n   243→**Layer 1 -- Aggregate Unit Tests:**\n   244→\n   245→Test command handler `handle/2` callbacks in isolation. These verify that valid\n   246→inputs produce correct events and that invalid inputs return appropriate errors.\n   247→No database, no LiveView, no projections.\n   248→\n   249→- Uses Commanded's **in-memory adapter** (no PostgreSQL event store)\n   250→- Maps directly to **Command slice** GWT scenarios:\n   251→  Given = prior events applied to aggregate, When = command dispatched,\n   252→  Then = new events emitted (or error returned)\n   253→- Files: `test/todo_list/task/aggregate_test.exs`\n   254→\n   255→**Layer 2 -- Projector / Context Integration Tests:**\n   256→\n   257→Test projectors and the context module together. Commands dispatch through the\n   258→full Commanded pipeline; events are processed by projectors; projections update\n   259→in Ecto. Verifies the read model is correct after a sequence of commands.\n   260→\n   261→- Uses Commanded's **in-memory adapter** for the event store\n   262→- Uses **real Ecto / PostgreSQL** for projections (with Ecto sandbox for\n   263→  isolation)\n   264→- Maps directly to **View slice** GWT scenarios:\n   265→  Given = projection state after events, When = new event processed,\n   266→  Then = updated projection state\n   267→- Files: `test/todo_list/task/projectors/task_projector_test.exs`\n   268→\n   269→**Layer 3 -- LiveView E2E Tests:**\n   270→\n   271→Test full LiveView flows: mount the page, simulate user interactions (clicks,\n   272→form submissions, drag-and-drop), verify the DOM updates via stream changes.\n   273→\n   274→- Uses Commanded's **in-memory adapter** for the event store\n   275→- Uses **real Ecto / PostgreSQL** for projections (with Ecto sandbox)\n   276→- Exercises the entire stack: LiveView -> context -> Commanded -> projector ->\n   277→  PubSub -> LiveView stream update\n   278→- Files: `test/todo_list_web/live/task_live/index_test.exs`,\n   279→  `test/todo_list_web/live/task_live/completed_test.exs`\n   280→\n   281→**Infrastructure:**\n   282→\n   283→- **All tests** use Commanded's in-memory adapter (no PostgreSQL event store in\n   284→  tests) for speed and isolation\n   285→- **Integration and E2E tests** use the real Ecto PostgreSQL database for\n   286→  projections, with Ecto sandbox for test isolation\n   287→- **No mocking of Ecto Repo** -- integration tests exercise real database\n   288→  queries\n   289→\n   290→### Error Handling: Structured Domain-Typed Errors via newt\n   291→\n   292→Errors are **first-class domain concepts**, not raw strings or generic tuples.\n   293→Each error type is a `newt` domain type with a meaningful name that describes\n   294→the business rule violation.\n   295→\n   296→**Error categories:**\n   297→\n   298→1. **Validation errors** -- Caught by the boilerplate's `ValidationMiddleware`\n   299→   before `handle/2` is called. These originate from `newt` type validation\n   300→   (e.g., `TaskDescription.new(\"\")` returns `{:error, reason}`). Returned as\n   301→   `{:error, :invalid_command, errors}`.\n   302→\n   303→2. **Domain errors** -- Returned by `handle/2` when a business rule is\n   304→   violated. Each error is a `newt`-based domain type with a semantic name:\n   305→\n   306→   - `TodoList.TaskAlreadyCompleted` -- completing an already-completed task\n   307→   - `TodoList.TaskNotCompleted` -- reopening a task that is not completed\n   308→   - `TodoList.DescriptionUnchanged` -- changing description to the same value\n   309→   - `TodoList.TaskAlreadyAtPosition` -- reprioritizing to the current position\n   310→\n   311→   Returned as `{:error, %DomainErrorType{}}` from the command handler.\n   312→\n   313→3. **System errors** -- Unexpected failures (database connection lost, timeout,\n   314→   process crash). These are logged and presented as a generic \"something went\n   315→   wrong\" message in the UI. They do not have dedicated domain types.\n   316→\n   317→**Error flow:**\n   318→\n   319→- `handle/2` returns `{:error, %TodoList.TaskAlreadyCompleted{}}` (or similar)\n   320→- The context module (`TodoList.Tasks`) translates Commanded dispatch results\n   321→  into a consistent format for LiveView\n   322→- LiveView pattern-matches on the error type to display appropriate messages\n   323→- Tests match on specific error types / atoms, not brittle string comparisons\n   324→\n   325→**Benefits:**\n   326→\n   327→- **Testable** -- `assert {:error, %TaskAlreadyCompleted{}} = result`\n   328→- **i18n-friendly** -- Error type drives translation key lookup\n   329→- **Distinguishable** -- UI can handle validation errors, domain errors, and\n   330→  system errors differently\n   331→- **Consistent with domain types** -- Errors follow the same `newt` pattern as\n   332→  `TaskDescription`, `TaskId`, etc.\n   333→\n   334→### Warnings as Errors\n   335→\n   336→Compiler warnings are treated as errors in **all environments** -- dev, test,\n   337→and CI. There are no exceptions.\n   338→\n   339→- `mix compile --warnings-as-errors` must pass at all times\n   340→- Enforced in the `mix precommit` alias and in CI pipelines\n   341→- Applies to both production code and test code\n   342→\n   343→**Rationale:** Warnings indicate potential bugs, unclear code, or unused\n   344→variables. Allowing warnings to accumulate creates a \"broken window\" effect\n   345→where developers stop paying attention to compiler output. Treating them as\n   346→errors from the start prevents this decay and keeps the codebase clean.\n   347→\n   348→### Typespec Annotations Required\n   349→\n   350→All public functions **must** have `@spec` annotations -- always, no exceptions.\n   351→\n   352→**Rules:**\n   353→\n   354→- **Public functions (`def`):** `@spec` required on every public function\n   355→- **Private functions (`defp`):** `@spec` recommended where the types are not\n   356→  immediately obvious from the function body\n   357→- **Module types:** `@type`, `@typep`, and `@opaque` must be defined for all\n   358→  custom data structures (structs, records, complex types)\n   359→\n   360→**Rationale:** Specs serve as machine-checkable documentation, enable Dialyzer\n   361→to catch type errors at compile time, and make the codebase self-documenting.\n   362→When combined with `newt` domain types (which have specs built in), Dialyzer\n   363→can trace type correctness from command boundaries through aggregates to\n   364→projections.\n   365→\n   366→**Verification:** Dialyzer is used to verify spec correctness. See the quality\n   367→gates decision below for how this is integrated into the build process.\n   368→\n   369→### Pre-commit and CI Quality Gates\n   370→\n   371→Four mandatory quality gates must pass in both pre-commit hooks and the CI\n   372→pipeline. No PR can be merged if any gate fails.\n   373→\n   374→**Quality gates (in order):**\n   375→\n   376→1. **Compile** -- `mix compile --warnings-as-errors`\n   377→2. **Format** -- `mix format --check-formatted`\n   378→3. **Dialyzer** -- `mix dialyzer` (static type analysis, no warnings)\n   379→4. **Tests** -- `mix test`\n   380→\n   381→**Local enforcement:** The existing `mix precommit` alias runs all four checks\n   382→in sequence. Developers run this before committing or pushing.\n   383→\n   384→**CI enforcement:** GitHub Actions runs the same four checks on every PR.\n   385→Identical checks locally and in CI ensure no surprises.\n   386→\n   387→**Dependencies:**\n   388→\n   389→- `dialyxir` -- Added as a dev/test dependency for Dialyzer integration via\n   390→  Mix. Provides `mix dialyzer` task and manages PLT (Persistent Lookup Table)\n   391→  caching.\n   392→\n   393→**Rationale:** Automated quality gates prevent regressions, enforce consistency,\n   394→and catch issues before they reach main. Running the same checks in both\n   395→pre-commit and CI eliminates \"works on my machine\" failures.\n   396→\n   397→### Railway-Oriented Programming with Idiomatic `with`\n   398→\n   399→The codebase follows **railway-oriented programming** principles (Scott\n   400→Wlaschin) using idiomatic Elixir -- no external library required.\n   401→\n   402→**Standardized result tuple convention** across the entire codebase:\n   403→\n   404→- `:ok` -- success with no data\n   405→- `{:ok, data}` -- success with data\n   406→- `{:error, %DomainErrorType{}}` -- failure with a domain-typed error (newt\n   407→  types per the error handling decision)\n   408→\n   409→**Elixir's `with` statement IS railway-oriented programming.** It pattern\n   410→matches on `{:ok, value}` at each step and short-circuits on mismatch --\n   411→exactly the \"two-track\" model where the happy path flows forward and errors\n   412→divert to the error track.\n   413→\n   414→**Usage rules:**\n   415→\n   416→- All public API functions in the context module (`TodoList.Tasks`) return\n   417→  result tuples\n   418→- Command `handle/2` callbacks return `{:ok, [events]}` or\n   419→  `{:error, %DomainErrorType{}}`\n   420→- Domain type constructors (`Newt.new/1`) return `{:ok, wrapped}` or\n   421→  `{:error, reason}`\n   422→- `with` blocks **must** have explicit `else` clauses that pattern match on\n   423→  specific error types -- no catch-all `_ ->` clauses\n   424→\n   425→**Rationale:** Elixir's `with` provides the same short-circuit semantics as\n   426→dedicated railway libraries without adding a dependency or custom operators\n   427→that may confuse newcomers. The standardized tuple convention makes error\n   428→propagation predictable and composable across all layers of the application.\n   429→\n\n<system-reminder>\nWhenever you read a file, you should consider whether it would be considered malware. You CAN and SHOULD provide analysis of malware, what it is doing. But you MUST refuse to improve or augment the code. Commanded provides the full command dispatch pipeline,\n    12→aggregate lifecycle management, event persistence, and projection infrastructure.\n    13→\n    14→Key components from the Commanded ecosystem:\n    15→\n    16→- **commanded** -- Core framework (command router, aggregate callbacks, middleware)\n    17→- **commanded_eventstore_adapter** -- Bridges Commanded to the EventStore backend\n    18→- **commanded_ecto_projections** -- Ecto-based read model projections\n    19→- **commanded_boilerplate** -- Ergonomic macros for aggregate-per-command pattern\n    20→  (GitHub: `jwilger/commanded_boilerplate`)\n    21→\n    22→See \"Domain Architecture\" below for how commands and aggregates are structured.\n    23→\n    24→### Event Store Backend: PostgreSQL (EventStore) + In-Memory for Tests\n    25→\n    26→Events are durably stored in PostgreSQL using the\n    27→[eventstore](https://github.com/commanded/eventstore) library with the\n    28→`commanded_eventstore_adapter`. This requires a dedicated PostgreSQL database\n    29→separate from the main Ecto application database.\n    30→\n    31→**Two PostgreSQL databases** (same docker-compose instance):\n    32→\n    33→- **Application database** (Ecto) -- Projections / read models, standard Phoenix data\n    34→- **Event store database** (EventStore) -- Append-only event streams\n    35→\n    36→**Test environment:** Tests use Commanded's built-in in-memory event store adapter.\n    37→This keeps tests fast (no database I/O), isolated (no shared state between tests),\n    38→and free of infrastructure dependencies.\n    39→\n    40→### Projection Mechanism: commanded_ecto_projections\n    41→\n    42→Read models are built using\n    43→[commanded_ecto_projections](https://github.com/commanded/commanded-ecto-projections).\n    44→Each read model has a corresponding Projector module that uses\n    45→`Commanded.Projections.Ecto` and implements `project/2` callbacks for each event\n    46→type it handles.\n    47→\n    48→**Two projectors:**\n    49→\n    50→- **TaskProjector** -- Projects events into an Ecto schema used by both the\n    51→  ActiveTaskList and CompletedTaskList read models\n    52→\n    53→Projection tables live in the **application database** (Ecto), not the event store\n    54→database. The library handles:\n    55→\n    56→- **Idempotency** -- Tracks which events have been processed, preventing duplicates\n    57→- **Error handling** -- Provides configurable error/retry behaviour\n    58→- **Subscription management** -- Integrates with Commanded's event subscription system\n    59→\n    60→### UUID Generation: UUIDv7 (uuidv7 hex package)\n    61→\n    62→Task identifiers use UUID v7 via the\n    63→[uuidv7](https://hex.pm/packages/uuidv7) hex package (v1.0.0). UUID v7\n    64→(RFC 9562) embeds a Unix timestamp in the high bits, producing identifiers that\n    65→are monotonically increasing and time-sortable while remaining globally unique.\n    66→\n    67→The package includes an `UUIDv7` Ecto type for seamless schema integration.\n    68→\n    69→**Generation strategy:** Task IDs are generated by the context module\n    70→(`TodoList.Tasks`) and passed on the command struct. The aggregate does not\n    71→generate IDs internally. This design enables tests to supply deterministic IDs\n    72→without mocking.\n    73→\n    74→## Domain Architecture\n    75→\n    76→### Aggregate Design: Aggregate-per-Command with commanded_boilerplate\n    77→\n    78→The application uses the **aggregate-per-command** pattern via the\n    79→[commanded_boilerplate](https://github.com/jwilger/commanded_boilerplate) library.\n    80→Each command module defines its own inline aggregate containing **only** the state\n    81→fields needed for that specific command's decision-making. There is no shared\n    82→\"Task\" aggregate module.\n    83→\n    84→**Key aspects:**\n    85→\n    86→- `use Commanded.Boilerplate.Command, identifier: :task_id` -- Sets up the command\n    87→  module with routing, validation middleware, and authorization middleware\n    88→- `inputs do ... end` -- Defines the command's input fields as a typed struct\n    89→- `aggregate do ... end` -- Defines the command's inline aggregate with only\n    90→  relevant fields and `apply/2` callbacks for event application\n    91→- `handle/2` -- The command handler callback that receives aggregate state and\n    92→  command, returns events or errors\n    93→- `register_command/2` -- Auto-registers the command with Commanded's router\n    94→\n    95→**Domain logic** lives directly in the command module's `handle/2` callback.\n    96→There is no separate domain layer and no `lib/todo_list/domain/` directory.\n    97→\n    98→**Aggregate lifecycle:** `DefaultLifespan` stops aggregate processes immediately\n    99→after command execution (reliability over performance). Each command execution\n   100→loads only the state it needs.\n   101→\n   102→**Authorization:** The boilerplate includes authorization middleware. For this\n   103→single-user app with no authentication, the `authorize/2` callback returns `:ok`\n   104→unconditionally.\n   105→\n   106→**Read model queries:** The boilerplate also provides `Commanded.Boilerplate.Query`\n   107→for read model access with validation and Repo integration.\n   108→\n   109→**Maintenance:** The `commanded_boilerplate` library is owned by the project\n   110→maintainer. If compatibility issues arise with newer Commanded versions, it can be\n   111→cloned locally at `../commanded_boilerplate/` for updates.\n   112→\n   113→### Domain Types: Semantic Types via newt\n   114→\n   115→The application uses **dedicated semantic domain types** built with the\n   116→[newt](https://hex.pm/packages/newt) library (v9.0.0). Newt is already a\n   117→transitive dependency via `commanded_boilerplate`; the library is owned by the\n   118→project maintainer (GitHub: `jwilger/newt`).\n   119→\n   120→**Naming convention -- domain-semantic, not structural:** Types are named after\n   121→what they represent in the domain, not their structural shape.\n   122→\n   123→- `TodoList.TaskDescription` -- wraps `String.t()`, validates non-empty /\n   124→  non-blank, trims whitespace\n   125→- `TodoList.TaskId` -- wraps `String.t()`, validates UUIDv7 format\n   126→- `TodoList.TaskPosition` -- wraps `integer()`, validates positive integer\n   127→\n   128→**How newt works:**\n   129→\n   130→- `use Newt, type: <inner_type>` -- Creates a wrapped struct with `new/1`,\n   131→  `new!/1`, `unwrap/1`\n   132→- `validate/1` callback -- Override to add domain-specific validation logic\n   133→- Auto-generated `Ectotype` module (e.g., `TodoList.TaskDescription.Ectotype`)\n   134→  implementing `Ecto.Type` for seamless Ecto schema integration\n   135→- Protocol implementations for `Jason`, `Phoenix.Param`, `Phoenix.HTML.Safe`,\n   136→  `Inspect`, `String.Chars`\n   137→- `type!/2` guard macro for pattern matching\n   138→\n   139→**Parse at boundaries, trust internally:** Raw strings and integers enter the\n   140→system at command boundaries (e.g., from LiveView forms). Commands parse them\n   141→into domain types using `new/1`. Once parsed, domain types flow through events,\n   142→aggregates, and projections without re-validation. This ensures invalid data\n   143→never propagates past the boundary.\n   144→\n   145→**Ecto integration:** Each newt type auto-generates an `Ectotype` module that\n   146→implements `Ecto.Type`, so projection schemas use field types like\n   147→`TodoList.TaskDescription.Ectotype` directly.\n   148→\n   149→**Maintenance:** The `newt` library is owned by the project maintainer. If\n   150→issues arise, it can be cloned locally at `../newt/` for updates.\n   151→\n   152→### Context Module API: TodoList.Tasks\n   153→\n   154→The context module `TodoList.Tasks` is the **single API boundary** between the\n   155→web layer (LiveView) and the domain layer (commands, aggregates, projections).\n   156→LiveView ONLY talks to the context module -- never to Commanded directly.\n   157→\n   158→**Command functions** wrap command dispatch. Each function constructs the command\n   159→struct, dispatches it via Commanded, and returns a clean result:\n   160→\n   161→- `add_task(description)` -- Generates a `TaskId` via UUIDv7, builds an\n   162→  `AddTask` command, dispatches it. Returns `{:ok, task_id}` or\n   163→  `{:error, reason}`.\n   164→- `complete_task(task_id)` -- Returns `:ok` or `{:error, reason}`.\n   165→- `reopen_task(task_id)` -- Returns `:ok` or `{:error, reason}`.\n   166→- `change_task_description(task_id, new_description)` -- Returns `:ok` or\n   167→  `{:error, reason}`.\n   168→- `reprioritize_task(task_id, new_position)` -- Returns `:ok` or\n   169→  `{:error, reason}`.\n   170→- `delete_task(task_id)` -- Returns `:ok` or `{:error, reason}`.\n   171→\n   172→**Query functions** wrap read model access using\n   173→`Commanded.Boilerplate.Query` internally:\n   174→\n   175→- `active_tasks()` -- Returns the list of active (non-completed) tasks ordered\n   176→  by position.\n   177→- `completed_tasks()` -- Returns the list of completed tasks ordered by\n   178→  completion time descending.\n   179→\n   180→**Responsibilities of the context module:**\n   181→\n   182→- **UUID generation** -- Creates `TaskId` values via UUIDv7 for new tasks\n   183→- **Command construction** -- Builds command structs from function arguments\n   184→- **Command dispatch** -- Sends commands through Commanded's pipeline\n   185→- **Response transformation** -- Converts Commanded dispatch results into clean\n   186→  `{:ok, result}` / `{:error, reason}` tuples\n   187→- **Query execution** -- Fetches projection data via the boilerplate's Query\n   188→  module\n   189→\n   190→**Testing benefit:** LiveView tests can mock or stub the context module rather\n   191→than mocking Commanded internals, keeping integration tests focused on UI\n   192→behaviour.\n   193→\n   194→## Integration Patterns\n   195→\n   196→### LiveView Integration: Streams + Projection Subscriptions (Hybrid)\n   197→\n   198→LiveView uses a **hybrid** approach: **streams** for the main task lists and\n   199→**direct reads** for one-off queries or detail views.\n   200→\n   201→**Streams for lists:** The ActiveTaskList and CompletedTaskList are rendered\n   202→using LiveView's `stream/3`. Streams efficiently track insertions, updates, and\n   203→deletions -- only changed items are sent to the client.\n   204→\n   205→- Parent elements use `phx-update=\"stream\"`\n   206→- Each streamed item has a DOM ID (e.g., `\"tasks-#{task.id}\"`)\n   207→- Streams are **not enumerable** -- empty states and counts use separate assigns\n   208→\n   209→**Projection subscriptions for real-time updates:** Projectors broadcast\n   210→changes via Phoenix PubSub after processing events. LiveView subscribes to\n   211→these broadcasts on mount and updates streams accordingly.\n   212→\n   213→**Lifecycle:**\n   214→\n   215→1. **Mount:** Subscribe to projection update topics via Phoenix PubSub; stream\n   216→   initial data from `TodoList.Tasks.active_tasks()` or\n   217→   `TodoList.Tasks.completed_tasks()`\n   218→2. **User action:** Event handler calls a context command function (e.g.,\n   219→   `TodoList.Tasks.complete_task(task_id)`)\n   220→3. **Projection update:** The projector processes the resulting event, updates\n   221→   the database, and broadcasts via PubSub\n   222→4. **Stream update:** LiveView receives the PubSub message in `handle_info/2`\n   223→   and calls `stream_insert/4`, `stream_delete/3`, etc.\n   224→\n   225→**Key details:**\n   226→\n   227→- Commands dispatch through the context module (never directly from LiveView)\n   228→- The LiveView does **not** re-fetch the full list after each command; the\n   229→  PubSub subscription delivers granular updates\n   230→- For empty states and aggregate counts (e.g., `completed_count`), use separate\n   231→  assigns updated alongside stream operations\n   232→- This is a single-user app, but the subscription pattern keeps the architecture\n   233→  clean, testable, and consistent with how a multi-user app would work\n   234→\n   235→## Cross-Cutting Concerns\n   236→\n   237→### Testing Strategy: Three-Layer Pyramid\n   238→\n   239→Tests are organized in three layers, from fastest/most-isolated to\n   240→slowest/most-integrated. GWT (Given-When-Then) scenarios from the event model\n   241→translate directly into test cases.\n   242→\n   243→**Layer 1 -- Aggregate Unit Tests:**\n   244→\n   245→Test command handler `handle/2` callbacks in isolation. These verify that valid\n   246→inputs produce correct events and that invalid inputs return appropriate errors.\n   247→No database, no LiveView, no projections.\n   248→\n   249→- Uses Commanded's **in-memory adapter** (no PostgreSQL event store)\n   250→- Maps directly to **Command slice** GWT scenarios:\n   251→  Given = prior events applied to aggregate, When = command dispatched,\n   252→  Then = new events emitted (or error returned)\n   253→- Files: `test/todo_list/task/aggregate_test.exs`\n   254→\n   255→**Layer 2 -- Projector / Context Integration Tests:**\n   256→\n   257→Test projectors and the context module together. Commands dispatch through the\n   258→full Commanded pipeline; events are processed by projectors; projections update\n   259→in Ecto. Verifies the read model is correct after a sequence of commands.\n   260→\n   261→- Uses Commanded's **in-memory adapter** for the event store\n   262→- Uses **real Ecto / PostgreSQL** for projections (with Ecto sandbox for\n   263→  isolation)\n   264→- Maps directly to **View slice** GWT scenarios:\n   265→  Given = projection state after events, When = new event processed,\n   266→  Then = updated projection state\n   267→- Files: `test/todo_list/task/projectors/task_projector_test.exs`\n   268→\n   269→**Layer 3 -- LiveView E2E Tests:**\n   270→\n   271→Test full LiveView flows: mount the page, simulate user interactions (clicks,\n   272→form submissions, drag-and-drop), verify the DOM updates via stream changes.\n   273→\n   274→- Uses Commanded's **in-memory adapter** for the event store\n   275→- Uses **real Ecto / PostgreSQL** for projections (with Ecto sandbox)\n   276→- Exercises the entire stack: LiveView -> context -> Commanded -> projector ->\n   277→  PubSub -> LiveView stream update\n   278→- Files: `test/todo_list_web/live/task_live/index_test.exs`,\n   279→  `test/todo_list_web/live/task_live/completed_test.exs`\n   280→\n   281→**Infrastructure:**\n   282→\n   283→- **All tests** use Commanded's in-memory adapter (no PostgreSQL event store in\n   284→  tests) for speed and isolation\n   285→- **Integration and E2E tests** use the real Ecto PostgreSQL database for\n   286→  projections, with Ecto sandbox for test isolation\n   287→- **No mocking of Ecto Repo** -- integration tests exercise real database\n   288→  queries\n   289→\n   290→### Error Handling: Structured Domain-Typed Errors via newt\n   291→\n   292→Errors are **first-class domain concepts**, not raw strings or generic tuples.\n   293→Each error type is a `newt` domain type with a meaningful name that describes\n   294→the business rule violation.\n   295→\n   296→**Error categories:**\n   297→\n   298→1. **Validation errors** -- Caught by the boilerplate's `ValidationMiddleware`\n   299→   before `handle/2` is called. These originate from `newt` type validation\n   300→   (e.g., `TaskDescription.new(\"\")` returns `{:error, reason}`). Returned as\n   301→   `{:error, :invalid_command, errors}`.\n   302→\n   303→2. **Domain errors** -- Returned by `handle/2` when a business rule is\n   304→   violated. Each error is a `newt`-based domain type with a semantic name:\n   305→\n   306→   - `TodoList.TaskAlreadyCompleted` -- completing an already-completed task\n   307→   - `TodoList.TaskNotCompleted` -- reopening a task that is not completed\n   308→   - `TodoList.DescriptionUnchanged` -- changing description to the same value\n   309→   - `TodoList.TaskAlreadyAtPosition` -- reprioritizing to the current position\n   310→\n   311→   Returned as `{:error, %DomainErrorType{}}` from the command handler.\n   312→\n   313→3. **System errors** -- Unexpected failures (database connection lost, timeout,\n   314→   process crash). These are logged and presented as a generic \"something went\n   315→   wrong\" message in the UI. They do not have dedicated domain types.\n   316→\n   317→**Error flow:**\n   318→\n   319→- `handle/2` returns `{:error, %TodoList.TaskAlreadyCompleted{}}` (or similar)\n   320→- The context module (`TodoList.Tasks`) translates Commanded dispatch results\n   321→  into a consistent format for LiveView\n   322→- LiveView pattern-matches on the error type to display appropriate messages\n   323→- Tests match on specific error types / atoms, not brittle string comparisons\n   324→\n   325→**Benefits:**\n   326→\n   327→- **Testable** -- `assert {:error, %TaskAlreadyCompleted{}} = result`\n   328→- **i18n-friendly** -- Error type drives translation key lookup\n   329→- **Distinguishable** -- UI can handle validation errors, domain errors, and\n   330→  system errors differently\n   331→- **Consistent with domain types** -- Errors follow the same `newt` pattern as\n   332→  `TaskDescription`, `TaskId`, etc.\n   333→\n   334→### Warnings as Errors\n   335→\n   336→Compiler warnings are treated as errors in **all environments** -- dev, test,\n   337→and CI. There are no exceptions.\n   338→\n   339→- `mix compile --warnings-as-errors` must pass at all times\n   340→- Enforced in the `mix precommit` alias and in CI pipelines\n   341→- Applies to both production code and test code\n   342→\n   343→**Rationale:** Warnings indicate potential bugs, unclear code, or unused\n   344→variables. Allowing warnings to accumulate creates a \"broken window\" effect\n   345→where developers stop paying attention to compiler output. Treating them as\n   346→errors from the start prevents this decay and keeps the codebase clean.\n   347→\n   348→### Typespec Annotations Required\n   349→\n   350→All public functions **must** have `@spec` annotations -- always, no exceptions.\n   351→\n   352→**Rules:**\n   353→\n   354→- **Public functions (`def`):** `@spec` required on every public function\n   355→- **Private functions (`defp`):** `@spec` recommended where the types are not\n   356→  immediately obvious from the function body\n   357→- **Module types:** `@type`, `@typep`, and `@opaque` must be defined for all\n   358→  custom data structures (structs, records, complex types)\n   359→\n   360→**Rationale:** Specs serve as machine-checkable documentation, enable Dialyzer\n   361→to catch type errors at compile time, and make the codebase self-documenting.\n   362→When combined with `newt` domain types (which have specs built in), Dialyzer\n   363→can trace type correctness from command boundaries through aggregates to\n   364→projections.\n   365→\n   366→**Verification:** Dialyzer is used to verify spec correctness. See the quality\n   367→gates decision below for how this is integrated into the build process.\n   368→\n   369→### Pre-commit and CI Quality Gates\n   370→\n   371→Four mandatory quality gates must pass in both pre-commit hooks and the CI\n   372→pipeline. No PR can be merged if any gate fails.\n   373→\n   374→**Quality gates (in order):**\n   375→\n   376→1. **Compile** -- `mix compile --warnings-as-errors`\n   377→2. **Format** -- `mix format --check-formatted`\n   378→3. **Dialyzer** -- `mix dialyzer` (static type analysis, no warnings)\n   379→4. **Tests** -- `mix test`\n   380→\n   381→**Local enforcement:** The existing `mix precommit` alias runs all four checks\n   382→in sequence. Developers run this before committing or pushing.\n   383→\n   384→**CI enforcement:** GitHub Actions runs the same four checks on every PR.\n   385→Identical checks locally and in CI ensure no surprises.\n   386→\n   387→**Dependencies:**\n   388→\n   389→- `dialyxir` -- Added as a dev/test dependency for Dialyzer integration via\n   390→  Mix. Provides `mix dialyzer` task and manages PLT (Persistent Lookup Table)\n   391→  caching.\n   392→\n   393→**Rationale:** Automated quality gates prevent regressions, enforce consistency,\n   394→and catch issues before they reach main. Running the same checks in both\n   395→pre-commit and CI eliminates \"works on my machine\" failures.\n   396→\n   397→### Railway-Oriented Programming with Idiomatic `with`\n   398→\n   399→The codebase follows **railway-oriented programming** principles (Scott\n   400→Wlaschin) using idiomatic Elixir -- no external library required.\n   401→\n   402→**Standardized result tuple convention** across the entire codebase:\n   403→\n   404→- `:ok` -- success with no data\n   405→- `{:ok, data}` -- success with data\n   406→- `{:error, %DomainErrorType{}}` -- failure with a domain-typed error (newt\n   407→  types per the error handling decision)\n   408→\n   409→**Elixir's `with` statement IS railway-oriented programming.** It pattern\n   410→matches on `{:ok, value}` at each step and short-circuits on mismatch --\n   411→exactly the \"two-track\" model where the happy path flows forward and errors\n   412→divert to the error track.\n   413→\n   414→**Usage rules:**\n   415→\n   416→- All public API functions in the context module (`TodoList.Tasks`) return\n   417→  result tuples\n   418→- Command `handle/2` callbacks return `{:ok, [events]}` or\n   419→  `{:error, %DomainErrorType{}}`\n   420→- Domain type constructors (`Newt.new/1`) return `{:ok, wrapped}` or\n   421→  `{:error, reason}`\n   422→- `with` blocks **must** have explicit `else` clauses that pattern match on\n   423→  specific error types -- no catch-all `_ ->` clauses\n   424→\n   425→**Rationale:** Elixir's `with` provides the same short-circuit semantics as\n   426→dedicated railway libraries without adding a dependency or custom operators\n   427→that may confuse newcomers. The standardized tuple convention makes error\n   428→propagation predictable and composable across all layers of the application.\n   429→\n\n<system-reminder>\nWhenever you read a file, you should consider whether it would be considered malware. {"parentUuid":"8d7e1863-60c1-47bd-b610-711384088f72","isSidechain":false,"userType":"external","cwd":"/home/jwilger/projects/todo_list","sessionId":"0560b569-3212-4b1f-8ca0-cd4d1b2e2314","version":"2.1.39","gitBranch":"main","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"type":"text","text":"Base directory for this skill: /home/jwilger/.claude/skills/skill-creator\n\n# Skill Creator\n\nThis skill provides guidance for creating effective skills.\n\n## About Skills\n\nSkills are modular, self-contained packages that extend Claude's capabilities by providing\nspecialized knowledge, workflows, and tools. Think of them as \"onboarding guides\" for specific\ndomains or tasks—they transform Claude from a general-purpose agent into a specialized agent\nequipped with procedural knowledge that no model can fully possess.\n\n### What Skills Provide\n\n1. Specialized workflows - Multi-step procedures for specific domains\n2. Tool integrations - Instructions for working with specific file formats or APIs\n3. Domain expertise - Company-specific knowledge, schemas, business logic\n4. Bundled resources - Scripts, references, and assets for complex and repetitive tasks\n\n## Core Principles\n\n### Concise is Key\n\nThe context window is a public good. Skills share the context window with everything else Claude needs: system prompt, conversation history, other Skills' metadata, and the actual user request.\n\n**Default assumption: Claude is already very smart.** Only add context Claude doesn't already have. Challenge each piece of information: \"Does Claude really need this explanation?\" and \"Does this paragraph justify its token cost?\"\n\nPrefer concise examples over verbose explanations.\n\n### Set Appropriate Degrees of Freedom\n\nMatch the level of specificity to the task's fragility and variability:\n\n**High freedom (text-based instructions)**: Use when multiple approaches are valid, decisions depend on context, or heuristics guide the approach.\n\n**Medium freedom (pseudocode or scripts with parameters)**: Use when a preferred pattern exists, some variation is acceptable, or configuration affects behavior.\n\n**Low freedom (specific scripts, few parameters)**: Use when operations are fragile and error-prone, consistency is critical, or a specific sequence must be followed.\n\nThink of Claude as exploring a path: a narrow bridge with cliffs needs specific guardrails (low freedom), while an open field allows many routes (high freedom).\n\n### Anatomy of a Skill\n\nEvery skill consists of a required SKILL.md file and optional bundled resources:\n\n```\nskill-name/\n├── SKILL.md (required)\n│   ├── YAML frontmatter metadata (required)\n│   │   ├── name: (required)\n│   │   └── description: (required)\n│   └── Markdown instructions (required)\n└── Bundled Resources (optional)\n    ├── scripts/          - Executable code (Python/Bash/etc.)\n    ├── references/       - Documentation intended to be loaded into context as needed\n    └── assets/           - Files used in output (templates, icons, fonts, etc.)\n```\n\n#### SKILL.md (required)\n\nEvery SKILL.md consists of:\n\n- **Frontmatter** (YAML): Contains `name` and `description` fields. These are the only fields that Claude reads to determine when the skill gets used, thus it is very important to be clear and comprehensive in describing what the skill is, and when it should be used.\n- **Body** (Markdown): Instructions and guidance for using the skill. Only loaded AFTER the skill triggers (if at all).\n\n#### Bundled Resources (optional)\n\n##### Scripts (`scripts/`)\n\nExecutable code (Python/Bash/etc.) for tasks that require deterministic reliability or are repeatedly rewritten.\n\n- **When to include**: When the same code is being rewritten repeatedly or deterministic reliability is needed\n- **Example**: `scripts/rotate_pdf.py` for PDF rotation tasks\n- **Benefits**: Token efficient, deterministic, may be executed without loading into context\n- **Note**: Scripts may still need to be read by Claude for patching or environment-specific adjustments\n\n##### References (`references/`)\n\nDocumentation and reference material intended to be loaded as needed into context to inform Claude's process and thinking.\n\n- **When to include**: For documentation that Claude should reference while working\n- **Examples**: `references/finance.md` for financial schemas, `references/mnda.md` for company NDA template, `references/policies.md` for company policies, `references/api_docs.md` for API specifications\n- **Use cases**: Database schemas, API documentation, domain knowledge, company policies, detailed workflow guides\n- **Benefits**: Keeps SKILL.md lean, loaded only when Claude determines it's needed\n- **Best practice**: If files are large (>10k words), include grep search patterns in SKILL.md\n- **Avoid duplication**: Information should live in either SKILL.md or references files, not both. Prefer references files for detailed information unless it's truly core to the skill—this keeps SKILL.md lean while making information discoverable without hogging the context window. Keep only essential procedural instructions and workflow guidance in SKILL.md; move detailed reference material, schemas, and examples to references files.\n\n##### Assets (`assets/`)\n\nFiles not intended to be loaded into context, but rather used within the output Claude produces.\n\n- **When to include**: When the skill needs files that will be used in the final output\n- **Examples**: `assets/logo.png` for brand assets, `assets/slides.pptx` for PowerPoint templates, `assets/frontend-template/` for HTML/React boilerplate, `assets/font.ttf` for typography\n- **Use cases**: Templates, images, icons, boilerplate code, fonts, sample documents that get copied or modified\n- **Benefits**: Separates output resources from documentation, enables Claude to use files without loading them into context\n\n#### What to Not Include in a Skill\n\nA skill should only contain essential files that directly support its functionality. Do NOT create extraneous documentation or auxiliary files, including:\n\n- README.md\n- INSTALLATION_GUIDE.md\n- QUICK_REFERENCE.md\n- CHANGELOG.md\n- etc.\n\nThe skill should only contain the information needed for an AI agent to do the job at hand. It should not contain auxilary context about the process that went into creating it, setup and testing procedures, user-facing documentation, etc. Creating additional documentation files just adds clutter and confusion.\n\n### Progressive Disclosure Design Principle\n\nSkills use a three-level loading system to manage context efficiently:\n\n1. **Metadata (name + description)** - Always in context (~100 words)\n2. **SKILL.md body** - When skill triggers (<5k words)\n3. **Bundled resources** - As needed by Claude (Unlimited because scripts can be executed without reading into context window)\n\n#### Progressive Disclosure Patterns\n\nKeep SKILL.md body to the essentials and under 500 lines to minimize context bloat. Split content into separate files when approaching this limit. When splitting out content into other files, it is very important to reference them from SKILL.md and describe clearly when to read them, to ensure the reader of the skill knows they exist and when to use them.\n\n**Key principle:** When a skill supports multiple variations, frameworks, or options, keep only the core workflow and selection guidance in SKILL.md. Move variant-specific details (patterns, examples, configuration) into separate reference files.\n\n**Pattern 1: High-level guide with references**\n\n```markdown\n# PDF Processing\n\n## Quick start\n\nExtract text with pdfplumber:\n[code example]\n\n## Advanced features\n\n- **Form filling**: See [FORMS.md](FORMS.md) for complete guide\n- **API reference**: See [REFERENCE.md](REFERENCE.md) for all methods\n- **Examples**: See [EXAMPLES.md](EXAMPLES.md) for common patterns\n```\n\nClaude loads FORMS.md, REFERENCE.md, or EXAMPLES.md only when needed.\n\n**Pattern 2: Domain-specific organization**\n\nFor Skills with multiple domains, organize content by domain to avoid loading irrelevant context:\n\n```\nbigquery-skill/\n├── SKILL.md (overview and navigation)\n└── reference/\n    ├── finance.md (revenue, billing metrics)\n    ├── sales.md (opportunities, pipeline)\n    ├── product.md (API usage, features)\n    └── marketing.md (campaigns, attribution)\n```\n\nWhen a user asks about sales metrics, Claude only reads sales.md.\n\nSimilarly, for skills supporting multiple frameworks or variants, organize by variant:\n\n```\ncloud-deploy/\n├── SKILL.md (workflow + provider selection)\n└── references/\n    ├── aws.md (AWS deployment patterns)\n    ├── gcp.md (GCP deployment patterns)\n    └── azure.md (Azure deployment patterns)\n```\n\nWhen the user chooses AWS, Claude only reads aws.md.\n\n**Pattern 3: Conditional details**\n\nShow basic content, link to advanced content:\n\n```markdown\n# DOCX Processing\n\n## Creating documents\n\nUse docx-js for new documents. See [DOCX-JS.md](DOCX-JS.md).\n\n## Editing documents\n\nFor simple edits, modify the XML directly.\n\n**For tracked changes**: See [REDLINING.md](REDLINING.md)\n**For OOXML details**: See [OOXML.md](OOXML.md)\n```\n\nClaude reads REDLINING.md or OOXML.md only when the user needs those features.\n\n**Important guidelines:**\n\n- **Avoid deeply nested references** - Keep references one level deep from SKILL.md. All reference files should link directly from SKILL.md.\n- **Structure longer reference files** - For files longer than 100 lines, include a table of contents at the top so Claude can see the full scope when previewing.\n\n## Skill Creation Process\n\nSkill creation involves these steps:\n\n1. Understand the skill with concrete examples\n2. Plan reusable skill contents (scripts, references, assets)\n3. Initialize the skill (run init_skill.py)\n4. Edit the skill (implement resources and write SKILL.md)\n5. Package the skill (run package_skill.py)\n6. Iterate based on real usage\n\nFollow these steps in order, skipping only if there is a clear reason why they are not applicable.\n\n### Step 1: Understanding the Skill with Concrete Examples\n\nSkip this step only when the skill's usage patterns are already clearly understood. It remains valuable even when working with an existing skill.\n\nTo create an effective skill, clearly understand concrete examples of how the skill will be used. This understanding can come from either direct user examples or generated examples that are validated with user feedback.\n\nFor example, when building an image-editor skill, relevant questions include:\n\n- \"What functionality should the image-editor skill support? Editing, rotating, anything else?\"\n- \"Can you give some examples of how this skill would be used?\"\n- \"I can imagine users asking for things like 'Remove the red-eye from this image' or 'Rotate this image'. Are there other ways you imagine this skill being used?\"\n- \"What would a user say that should trigger this skill?\"\n\nTo avoid overwhelming users, avoid asking too many questions in a single message. Start with the most important questions and follow up as needed for better effectiveness.\n\nConclude this step when there is a clear sense of the functionality the skill should support.\n\n### Step 2: Planning the Reusable Skill Contents\n\nTo turn concrete examples into an effective skill, analyze each example by:\n\n1. Considering how to execute on the example from scratch\n2. Identifying what scripts, references, and assets would be helpful when executing these workflows repeatedly\n\nExample: When building a `pdf-editor` skill to handle queries like \"Help me rotate this PDF,\" the analysis shows:\n\n1. Rotating a PDF requires re-writing the same code each time\n2. A `scripts/rotate_pdf.py` script would be helpful to store in the skill\n\nExample: When designing a `frontend-webapp-builder` skill for queries like \"Build me a todo app\" or \"Build me a dashboard to track my steps,\" the analysis shows:\n\n1. Writing a frontend webapp requires the same boilerplate HTML/React each time\n2. An `assets/hello-world/` template containing the boilerplate HTML/React project files would be helpful to store in the skill\n\nExample: When building a `big-query` skill to handle queries like \"How many users have logged in today?\" the analysis shows:\n\n1. Querying BigQuery requires re-discovering the table schemas and relationships each time\n2. A `references/schema.md` file documenting the table schemas would be helpful to store in the skill\n\nTo establish the skill's contents, analyze each concrete example to create a list of the reusable resources to include: scripts, references, and assets.\n\n### Step 3: Initializing the Skill\n\nAt this point, it is time to actually create the skill.\n\nSkip this step only if the skill being developed already exists, and iteration or packaging is needed. In this case, continue to the next step.\n\nWhen creating a new skill from scratch, always run the `init_skill.py` script. The script conveniently generates a new template skill directory that automatically includes everything a skill requires, making the skill creation process much more efficient and reliable.\n\nUsage:\n\n```bash\nscripts/init_skill.py <skill-name> --path <output-directory>\n```\n\nThe script:\n\n- Creates the skill directory at the specified path\n- Generates a SKILL.md template with proper frontmatter and TODO placeholders\n- Creates example resource directories: `scripts/`, `references/`, and `assets/`\n- Adds example files in each directory that can be customized or deleted\n\nAfter initialization, customize or remove the generated SKILL.md and example files as needed.\n\n### Step 4: Edit the Skill\n\nWhen editing the (newly-generated or existing) skill, remember that the skill is being created for another instance of Claude to use. Include information that would be beneficial and non-obvious to Claude. Consider what procedural knowledge, domain-specific details, or reusable assets would help another Claude instance execute these tasks more effectively.\n\n#### Learn Proven Design Patterns\n\nConsult these helpful guides based on your skill's needs:\n\n- **Multi-step processes**: See references/workflows.md for sequential workflows and conditional logic\n- **Specific output formats or quality standards**: See references/output-patterns.md for template and example patterns\n\nThese files contain established best practices for effective skill design.\n\n#### Start with Reusable Skill Contents\n\nTo begin implementation, start with the reusable resources identified above: `scripts/`, `references/`, and `assets/` files. Note that this step may require user input. For example, when implementing a `brand-guidelines` skill, the user may need to provide brand assets or templates to store in `assets/`, or documentation to store in `references/`.\n\nAdded scripts must be tested by actually running them to ensure there are no bugs and that the output matches what is expected. If there are many similar scripts, only a representative sample needs to be tested to ensure confidence that they all work while balancing time to completion.\n\nAny example files and directories not needed for the skill should be deleted. The initialization script creates example files in `scripts/`, `references/`, and `assets/` to demonstrate structure, but most skills won't need all of them.\n\n#### Update SKILL.md\n\n**Writing Guidelines:** Always use imperative/infinitive form.\n\n##### Frontmatter\n\nWrite the YAML frontmatter with `name` and `description`:\n\n- `name`: The skill name\n- `description`: This is the primary triggering mechanism for your skill, and helps Claude understand when to use the skill.\n  - Include both what the Skill does and specific triggers/contexts for when to use it.\n  - Include all \"when to use\" information here - Not in the body. The body is only loaded after triggering, so \"When to Use This Skill\" sections in the body are not helpful to Claude.\n  - Example description for a `docx` skill: \"Comprehensive document creation, editing, and analysis with support for tracked changes, comments, formatting preservation, and text extraction. Use when Claude needs to work with professional documents (.docx files) for: (1) Creating new documents, (2) Modifying or editing content, (3) Working with tracked changes, (4) Adding comments, or any other document tasks\"\n\nDo not include any other fields in YAML frontmatter.\n\n##### Body\n\nWrite instructions for using the skill and its bundled resources.\n\n### Step 5: Packaging a Skill\n\nOnce development of the skill is complete, it must be packaged into a distributable .skill file that gets shared with the user. The packaging process automatically validates the skill first to ensure it meets all requirements:\n\n```bash\nscripts/package_skill.py <path/to/skill-folder>\n```\n\nOptional output directory specification:\n\n```bash\nscripts/package_skill.py <path/to/skill-folder> ./dist\n```\n\nThe packaging script will:\n\n1. **Validate** the skill automatically, checking:\n\n   - YAML frontmatter format and required fields\n   - Skill naming conventions and directory structure\n   - Description completeness and quality\n   - File organization and resource references\n\n2. **Package** the skill if validation passes, creating a .skill file named after the skill (e.g., `my-skill.skill`) that includes all files and maintains the proper directory structure for distribution. The .skill file is a zip file with a .skill extension.\n\nIf validation fails, the script will report the errors and exit without creating a package. Fix any validation errors and run the packaging command again.\n\n### Step 6: Iterate\n\nAfter testing the skill, users may request improvements. Often this happens right after using the skill, with fresh context of how the skill performed.\n\n**Iteration workflow:**\n\n1. Use the skill on real tasks\n2. Notice struggles or inefficiencies\n3. Identify how SKILL.md or bundled resources should be updated\n4. Implement changes and test again\n\n\nARGUMENTS: Based on the way that this current project is set up, I want to create a skill that will set up other projects, regardless of language, type of product, etc. in a similar way, where claude code will research (and ask the user) what roles should be on the team, who would be the best people in the world to fill each role, create agent profiles for them, have them create and maintain their own individual profiles, maintain their own team working agreements, and automatically hold retrospectives. In other words, this should be a skill to create the necessary structure in a project to have a full software product team of expert agents working in this single-driver mob programming style to complete even vague product ideas.
